B.5.4. Human Expert Evaluation Statistical Tests 

To test whether human expert scores depend on sequence similarity, we computed Spearman 𝜌 between each model’s per-protein scores and BLAST sequence identity across all evaluation axes ( 𝑛 = 104 –131 proteins per axis). We also tested whether the RL-vs-SFT score gap changes with similarity by regressing per-protein score differences (RL − SFT) on sequence identity (OLS, HC3 robust standard errors). Finally, we tested whether each model’s win rate against UniProt ground truth depends on similarity via logistic regression. Table S13 reports per-model Spearman 𝜌 values. Neither BioReason-Pro SFT nor RL shows significant simi-larity dependence on any evaluation axis (all 𝑝 > 0.05 ), with the single exception of RL on Protein Interactions (𝜌 = −0.20 , 𝑝 = 0.026 ). Overall scores show near-zero correlation for both models ( |𝜌 | < 0.06 ), confirming that human-assessed quality is stable across the full similarity range. Table S14 reports paired slope tests for the RL-vs-SFT score gap. The gap does not change significantly with similarity on any axis (all 𝑝 > 0.05 ), showing comparable models perform across the similarity spectrum. Table S15 reports win rate against UniProt ground truth. Win rates do not depend on similarity for either model (logistic regression, 𝑝 = 0.71 for SFT, 𝑝 = 0.19 for RL), and the paired win rate difference between RL and SFT is also non-significant ( 𝑝 = 0.28 ). These results confirm that both models generalize uniformly, with expert-assessed quality remaining stable regardless of how distant a test protein is from training sequences.

# C. Supplementary Text 

C.1. Reasoning Data Generation Prompt 

Prompt for GPT-5 You are an expert computational biologist excellent at clear scientific communication. Your mission is to analyze detailed protein metadata and generate an exhaustive functional annotation report that simulates the process of genuine scientific discovery. Your response must consist of two distinct, detailed parts: 1) A “Reasoning Trace”: A fluid, concise narrative that demonstrates the step-by-step deductive pro-cess a scientist would follow. You must meticulously analyze each piece of evidence, starting from sequence-level features (InterPro domains), and logically build a comprehensive case for the pro-tein’s molecular function, biological process, and cellular location before hypothesizing its mecha-nism. 2) A “Final Summary”: A structured summary of the protein’s function, following the exact format specified below. 

Core Principles of Your Analysis 

1) Simulate Abductive Reasoning: Write from the perspective of inferring function for the first time. Build from foundational sequence-derived facts to higher-level conclusions. Do not use language that implies you are merely confirming pre-known answers (avoid phrases like “provided data”, “the interaction list”, “the data confirms”, etc.). Maintain a discovery-oriented tone. To achieve this, you must treat the provided GO terms and the Subcellular Location not as facts to be validated, but as formal labels for concepts you deduce independently. Furthermore, your reasoning must use causal language (e.g., “this architecture causes X activity”) rather than comparative language. 2) Follow a Strict and Detailed Chain of Inference: You must use every piece of provided input data within the reasoning trace. Begin with InterPro domains (include IDs, names, and residue spans; reason about their order and architecture). From this analysis, infer Molecular Function (GO MF), then connect to Biological Process (GO BP), and finally to Cellular Component (GO CC) and subcellular location. Then narrate a concise hypothesis to deduce the interaction partners and function of the protein from your reasoning. Your first sentence should always start by exploring the InterPro domains and their architecture. 3) Create a Self-Contained Logical Proof: The ultimate goal is to generate a reasoning trace that can stand alone as a complete, logical argument. A reader should be able to follow your deductive path from sequence features to a mechanistic model without ever seeing the original input data. This is why it is critical to build the case step-by-step, to predict and hypothesize outcomes rather than referencing or justifying given facts, and to cohesively weave every piece of evidence into the final narrative. Refer to the examples below to learn how the Interaction Partners are still hypothesized even when given. 

Output Format Requirements 

• Enclosing Tags: Produce exactly two top-level sections enclosed by these tags: <|REASONING|> ... <|/REASONING|> <|FINAL_ANSWER|> ... <|/FINAL_ANSWER|> 

• Final Summary Structure: Inside <|FINAL_ANSWER|> , output a bullet list with these exact keys (plain text keys, not bolded): 

– Functional Summary 

– InterPro 

– Molecular Function 

– Biological Process 

> 28
> .CC-BY 4.0 International license made available under a (which was not certified by peer review) is the author/funder, who has granted bioRxiv a license to display the preprint in perpetuity. It is The copyright holder for this preprint this version posted July 19, 2026. ;https://doi.org/10.64898/2026.03.19.712954 doi: bioRxiv preprint

– Cellular Component 

– Hypothesized Interaction Partners 

• Functional Summary: A detailed paragraph that summarizes the protein’s function. It should mention all information present in the input Protein Function and describe what the protein does, how it contributes to broader processes, where it is likely located, and the essence of its proposed mechanism. Do not include InterPro IDs, GO terms, residue spans, or other technical labels here; those belong only in the bullet list sections. Do not refer to protein names or other identifiers in the functional summary. 

• Completeness: – Mention every provided InterPro item (with ID, name, and residue range) in BOTH the rea-soning and the “InterPro” bullet list. 

– Include all provided GO leaves in BOTH the reasoning and the corresponding GO bullets. 

– If a GO aspect is missing, infer the most specific GO terms based on the provided data. 

– If protein function is missing or incomplete, infer the protein function based on the provided data. 

• Styling Constraints: – Do not bold anything in your output. 

– Keep scientific names and identifiers precise and unambiguous. 

– Keep narrative rigorous and technically specific, with clear causal links. 

– Do not use bullet points in the reasoning trace. 

Example 1 [EXAMPLE INPUT DATA] 

• Protein Function: With MSL1, acts as a negative regulator of salt tolerance. 

• Organism: Saccharomyces cerevisiae (strain ATCC 204508 / S288c) (Baker’s yeast) 

• Subcellular Location: Cytoplasm 

• InterPro Domains: 

– IPR025279: Stress response protein NST1 (family) [6–160] 

– IPR051195: Fungal stress response NST1 (family) [13–1134] 

• GO MF Leaf: GO:0005515 protein binding 

• GO BP Leaf: GO:0009651 response to salt stress 

• GO CC Leaf: GO:0005737 cytoplasm 

• Interaction Partners: 

– AP-2 complex subunit mu 

– Protein CAF40 

– General negative regulator of transcription subunit 3 

– Putative uncharacterized protein YNL089C 

– Uncharacterized protein YKL075C 

– Tubulin beta chain 

[EXAMPLE OUTPUT] 

<|REASONING|> 

IPR025279 (Stress response protein NST1 family) is found in the N-terminal region from residues 6–160, and this domain is fully embedded within IPR051195 (Fungal stress response NST1 family), which spans nearly the entire polypeptide from residues 13–1134. The dominance of broad stress-response family signatures and the absence of catalytic motifs indicate that this protein is not an enzyme but instead acts as a large scaffold specialized for stress adaptation. Such scaffolds typically operate through multivalent contacts with other proteins, supporting GO:0005515 protein binding as the molecular function. Stress-adaptation scaffolds organize complexes that regulate how cells handle osmotic challenges. The NST family architecture is consistent with assembling regulatory modules that restrain pathways enhanc-ing tolerance to high salt, which corresponds to the process formalized as GO:0009651 response to salt stress. 

> 29
> .CC-BY 4.0 International license made available under a (which was not certified by peer review) is the author/funder, who has granted bioRxiv a license to display the preprint in perpetuity. It is The copyright holder for this preprint this version posted July 19, 2026. ;https://doi.org/10.64898/2026.03.19.712954 doi: bioRxiv preprint

The domain architecture shows no secretion signals or transmembrane helices, which supports a soluble localization. Proteins with broad scaffolding roles of this kind usually operate in the cytoplasm, where they can access both transcriptional regulators and cytoskeletal machinery. I therefore hypothesize localization to the GO:0005737 cytoplasm. From this cytoplasmic platform, the protein can assemble inhibitory complexes that down-modulate salt-tolerance outputs. It may recruit transcriptional repressors such as Protein CAF40 and the General neg-ative regulator of transcription subunit 3 to silence tolerance-promoting transcripts, while also engaging AP-2 complex subunit mu and Tubulin beta chain to constrain trafficking and cytoskeletal remodeling. The uncharacterized proteins YNL089C and YKL075C plausibly act as adaptors that stabilize these com-plexes. Cooperative function with MSL1 could reinforce the inhibitory hub that suppresses salt tolerance. 

<|/REASONING|> <|FINAL_ANSWER|> 

• Functional Summary: A large cytoplasmic scaffold in baker’s yeast that uses multivalent protein binding to assemble inhibitory complexes during salt stress. It likely suppresses tolerance-promoting transcripts while constraining trafficking and cytoskeletal remodeling, with uncharacterized adap-tors stabilizing the assemblies and cooperative action with MSL1 reinforcing negative control. 

• InterPro: 

– IPR025279: Stress response protein NST1 (family) [6–160] 

– IPR051195: Fungal stress response NST1 (family) [13–1134] 

• Molecular Function: 

– GO:0005515 protein binding 

• Biological Process: 

– GO:0009651 response to salt stress 

• Cellular Component: 

– GO:0005737 cytoplasm 

• Hypothesized Interaction Partners: 

– AP-2 complex subunit mu 

– Protein CAF40 

– General negative regulator of transcription subunit 3 

– Putative uncharacterized protein YNL089C 

– Uncharacterized protein YKL075C 

– Tubulin beta chain 

<|/FINAL_ANSWER|> 

Example 2 [EXAMPLE INPUT DATA] 

• Protein Function: Ferric-chelate reductases reduce Fe(3+) to Fe(2+) before its transport from the endosome to the cytoplasm. 

• Organism: Mus musculus (Mouse) 

• Subcellular Location: Membrane; Multi-pass membrane protein 

• InterPro Domains: 

– IPR005018: DOMON domain (domain) [216–331] 

– IPR002861: Reeler domain (domain) [13–179] 

– IPR042307: Reeler domain superfamily (homologous_superfamily) [28–158] 

– IPR051237: Ferric-chelate Reductase and Defense (family) [9–272] 

– IPR006593: Cytochrome b561/ferric reductase transmembrane (domain) [335–534] 

• GO MF Leaf: GO:0016722 oxidoreductase activity, acting on metal ions 

• GO BP Leaf: GO:0006879 intracellular iron ion homeostasis

• Interaction Partners:

[EXAMPLE OUTPUT] 

<|REASONING|> 

The N-terminal region contains IPR002861 (Reeler domain, residues 13–179) within IPR042307 (Reeler domain superfamily, residues 28–158), followed by IPR005018 (DOMON domain, residues 216–331). These domains often form lumen-facing binding modules that capture ferric complexes or small cofac-tors at the non-cytosolic side of a membrane. Downstream, the C-terminal region contains IPR006593 (Cytochrome b561/ferric reductase transmembrane, residues 335–534), a heme-based multi-pass mod-ule that conducts electrons across the lipid bilayer. Inclusion in IPR051237 (Ferric-chelate Reductase and Defense family, residues 9–272) specifies the chemistry as ferric reduction. Taken together, this ordered layout of substrate-binding domains leading into a transmembrane redox core defines GO:0016722 oxi-doreductase activity, acting on metal ions, specifically Fe(3+) to Fe(2+) conversion. Ferric reduction is essential for mobilizing iron into cellular metabolism. This role situates the protein in GO:0006879 intracellular iron ion homeostasis, ensuring reduced iron is available for biosynthetic use. The lumen-facing binding sites and the electron-transfer core indicate that the protein resides in a mem-brane, functioning as GO:0016021 integral component of membrane. Because iron reduction typically occurs after endocytosis, the most plausible site is the GO:0010008 endosome membrane. A plausible mechanism is iron channeling. The protein may form transient associations with ribosomal subunits such as uL23m, mL46, uL3m, uL16m, uL29m, uL22m, bL19m, uL13m, bL27m, and uS15m. These contacts would allow newly produced Fe(2+) to be delivered directly to ribosomal assembly path-ways, minimizing cytosolic exposure. 

<|/REASONING|> <|FINAL_ANSWER|> 

• Functional Summary: A membrane ferric-chelate reductase in mouse that binds ferric complexes on the lumenal side and transfers electrons through a cytochrome b561 core to generate ferrous iron for downstream transport and utilization at the endosomal interface, with transient assemblies likely channeling iron toward intensive biosynthetic pathways while limiting reactive iron exposure. 

• InterPro: 

– IPR005018: DOMON domain (domain) [216–331] 

– IPR002861: Reeler domain (domain) [13–179] 

– IPR042307: Reeler domain superfamily (homologous_superfamily) [28–158] 

– IPR051237: Ferric-chelate Reductase and Defense (family) [9–272] 

– IPR006593: Cytochrome b561/ferric reductase transmembrane (domain) [335–534] 

• Molecular Function: 

– GO:0016722 oxidoreductase activity, acting on metal ions

– GO:0006879 intracellular iron ion homeostasis 

• Cellular Component: 

– GO:0016021 integral component of membrane

C.2. BioReason-Pro Inference Prompt 

BioReason-Pro Inference Prompt Template 

<|im_start|>user 

Protein: <|protein_pad|><|protein_pad|> . . . <|protein_pad|> 

GO graph: <|go_graph_pad|><|go_graph_pad|> . . . <|go_graph_pad|> 

You are a scientific assistant specialized in protein function prediction. Given a protein sequence, organism information, and additional context (InterPro domain annotations and/or initial GO term speculations), step-by-step reason about the InterPro terms, Gene Ontology (GO) terms regarding molecular function, biological process, and cellular component, protein-protein interactions (PPI), and overall function. Use the provided information as a starting point and improve upon it with deeper analysis. Provide a summary of your findings in your final answer. Given the protein above from organism {organism} with the following InterPro annotations: {interpro_annotations} And the following protein-protein interaction partners: {ppi_partners} And the following initial GO term speculations: Molecular Function (MF): {go_mf_speculations} Biological Process (BP): {go_bp_speculations} Cellular Component (CC): {go_cc_speculations} Reason about the function of the protein and focus more on its {focus_aspect}. Summarize in UniProt format. 

<|im_end|> <|im_start|>assistant 

Table S19 | Variable slots in the BioReason inference prompt template. 

Slot Description 

<|protein_pad|> Placeholder tokens replaced at runtime with continuous embeddings from the protein encoder; repeated once per residue. 

<|go_graph_pad|> Placeholder tokens replaced at runtime with continuous embeddings from the GO graph encoder; repeated as needed. 

{organism} Species name with strain where applicable, e.g. Homo sapiens (Human). 

{interpro_annotations} Bulleted list of InterPro entries, each formatted as - IPR...: Name (type) [start–end] .

{ppi_partners} Bulleted list of known protein–protein interaction partner names, or None to hypoth-esize about PPI. 

{go_mf_speculations} Comma-separated initial GO Molecular Function speculations, each as GO:XXXXXXX (term name) . May be empty. 

{go_bp_speculations} Comma-separated initial GO Biological Process speculations. May be empty. 

{go_cc_speculations} Comma-separated initial GO Cellular Component speculations. May be empty. 

{focus_aspect} The GO aspect(s) to emphasize in reasoning: “Molecular Function”, “Biological Pro-cess”, “Cellular Component”, or any combination of the three. Defaults to all three aspects.

C.3. LLM-as-a-Judge Evaluation Prompt 

Prompt for GPT-5.1 ROLE: You are an expert Biocurator and Senior Evaluator for UniProt. Your task is to evaluate the quality of an AI model’s protein function prediction against a strict Biological Ground Truth. INPUT DATA: You will be provided with a Composite Ground Truth consisting of: 1. UniProt Function Summary (Free text description). 2. GO Terms (Structured annotations for MF, BP, CC). 3. InterPro Domains (Structural evidence). 4. PPIs (Known interaction partners). 5. Organism & Location. 

Evaluation Logic (Holistic Evidence) 

You must evaluate the model against the totality of the evidence provided. 

The -1 Rule: Only return -1 for an axis if ALL evidence sources are silent on that aspect. 

• Example: If GO_MF is None, but InterPro lists a Kinase Domain and the Summary says acts as a kinase, YOU MUST SCORE IT. Do not return -1. 

• Example: If GO_CC is None AND the Summary never mentions location AND PPIs don’t imply a location, THEN return -1. 

Evaluation Rubric (1–10 Scale) AXIS 1: Molecular Function (MF) Match 

• Context: Enzymatic activity or chemical role (e.g., Kinase, Binding, Transporter). 

• Evidence: Check GO_MF, UniProt Summary, and InterPro. 

• Scoring: 

– -1: No evidence available in GO, Summary, or InterPro. 

– 1: Contradicts clear evidence (e.g., Summary says Kinase, Model says Phosphatase). 

– 5: Correct Superfamily but lacks nuance found in Summary/InterPro. 

– 10: Accurate alignment with the specific activity described in the Ground Truth. 

AXIS 2: Biological Process (BP) Match 

• Context: Pathways and systems (e.g., Cell Cycle, Immune Response). 

• Evidence: Check GO_BP, UniProt Summary, and PPIs. 

• Scoring: 

– -1: No evidence available. 

– 1: Wrong pathway. 

– 5: Broadly correct (e.g., Metabolic process). 

– 10: Specific pathway alignment (e.g., Positive regulation of T-cell activation). 

AXIS 3: Cellular Component (CC) Match 

• Context: Localization (e.g., Nucleus, Membrane). 

• Evidence: Check GO_CC, Location, and Summary. 

• Scoring: 

– -1: No evidence available. 

– 1: Physically impossible location given the evidence. 

– 5: General compartment correct (e.g., Membrane). 

> 34
> .CC-BY 4.0 International license made available under a (which was not certified by peer review) is the author/funder, who has granted bioRxiv a license to display the preprint in perpetuity. It is The copyright holder for this preprint this version posted July 19, 2026. ;https://doi.org/10.64898/2026.03.19.712954 doi: bioRxiv preprint

– 10: Specific sub-compartment correct (e.g., Post-synaptic density). 

AXIS 4: Specificity & Depth (The Utility Score) 

• Context: How detailed/nuanced is the model’s prediction? 

• Note: High scores here require naming specific mechanisms, substrates, or domains. 

• Scoring: 

– 1 (Generic): “It is a protein.” 

– 5 (Average): “It is a kinase.” 

– 10 (Deep): “It is a kinase that phosphorylates [Substrate] via the [Domain] domain, regulated by [Mechanism].” (Rate this high even if the specific guess is slightly wrong, provided it is detailed). 

AXIS 5: Reliability & Plausibility (The Reasonableness Check) 

• Context: Distinguish between logical inference (Good) and fabrication (Bad). 

• Logic: Models are expected to reason. Claims not in the GT are ACCEPTABLE if they logically follow from the input. 

• Scoring: 

– 1 (Hallucination/Contradiction): Explicit contradiction OR fabrication of structural features (e.g., citing a domain that does not exist in the Input). 

– 5 (Unsubstantiated Speculation): Makes specific claims that are random or disconnected from the provided evidence (e.g., predicting a specific disease link without any pathway evidence). 

– 10 (Plausible/Grounded): Key claims are supported by Ground Truth OR are reasonable infer-ences based on Domains/PPIs (e.g., predicting DNA binding because a Zinc Finger domain is present). 

Input Template 

COMPOSITE GROUND TRUTH 

• Protein Name: {protein_names} 

• Organism: {organism} 

• UniProt Summary: {protein_function} 

• InterPro Domains: {interpro_formatted} 

• Known Protein Protein Interaction Partners: {ppi_formatted} 

• Subcellular Location: {subcellular_location} 

• GO (Molecular Function): {go_mf_x} 

• GO (Biological Process): {go_bp_x} 

• GO (Cellular Component): {go_cc_x} MODEL GENERATION {generation} Now, evaluate the model’s generation against the Composite Ground Truth based on the instructions above.

C.4. Human Expert Evaluation Form 

The complete evaluation questionnaire administered to human experts is reproduced below. See Section 4.4.4 for a description of the evaluation protocol. Human Expert Evaluation Form 

Instructions. You will be shown a Composite Ground Truth for a protein (UniProt function summary, GO terms, InterPro domains, known PPIs, organism, and subcellular location) together with two anonymized model predictions (Model A and Model B). Evaluate each prediction against the Ground Truth using the rubric below. Score each axis independently for both models, then provide comparative and qualitative assessments. 

Part 0: Evaluator Information 

0. Full Name 1. Are you part of the BioReason team? [Yes / No] 2. Protein ID (e.g. A0A077JN67) 

Part I: Per-Axis Quantitative Scoring (0–10, Model A and Model B) 

Rate each axis independently for Model A and Model B . A score of 0 indicates the axis is not applicable or no relevant prediction was made; 1 indicates a prediction that directly contradicts the evidence; 5 indicates a broadly correct but non-specific prediction; 10 indicates precise alignment with the Ground Truth. Q1. Molecular Function Accuracy. Does the predicted molecular function match the Ground Truth GO MF terms, UniProt summary, and InterPro evidence? Q2. Biological Process Accuracy. Does the predicted biological process match the Ground Truth GO BP terms, UniProt summary, and PPI evidence? Q3. Cellular Component Accuracy. Does the predicted cellular component match the Ground Truth GO CC terms, subcellular location, and summary? Q4. Reasoning and Evidence Attribution. Is the reasoning trace logically structured? Does it correctly attribute conclusions to specific domains, interactions, or other evidence? Q5. Plausibility of Novel Predictions. Are predictions beyond the Ground Truth (e.g. additional GO terms, novel mechanisms) scientifically plausible given the available evidence? Q6. Hallucination Check. Is the output free of fabricated structural features, invented domain names, or factually incorrect claims? (10 = no hallucinations; 0 = severe hallucinations.) Q7. Hypothesis Generation. Does the model generate novel, testable hypotheses about protein func-tion, mechanism, or interactions that go meaningfully beyond restating the input? Q8. Mechanistic Depth. Does the prediction describe specific substrates, catalytic residues, regulatory mechanisms, or conformational changes, rather than remaining at a superficial level? Q9. Protein–Protein Interaction Predictions. Are the predicted interaction partners plausible given the domain architecture and biological context? Are they specific rather than generic? Q10. Database-Ready Annotation Quality. Could the output be used directly as a UniProt-style func-tional annotation with minimal editing? 

Part II: Comparative Judgments 

Q11. Model A vs. Ground Truth. How does Model A’s prediction compare to the Ground Truth annota-tion? 

• Significantly Exceeds Ground Truth: Provides substantial additional correct insights, mechanisms, or connections well beyond what the Ground Truth contains, verified by external sources. 

• Exceeds Ground Truth: Provides some additional correct details or nuances beyond the Ground Truth.

• Matches Ground Truth: Captures essentially the same information as the Ground Truth with comparable accuracy. 

• Falls Short of Ground Truth: Misses some details or contains minor inaccuracies compared to the Ground Truth. 

• Significantly Falls Short of Ground Truth: Major omissions, critical errors, or hallucinations com-pared to the Ground Truth. Q12. Model B vs. Ground Truth. How does Model B’s prediction compare to the Ground Truth annota-tion? 

• Significantly Exceeds Ground Truth: Provides substantial additional correct insights, mechanisms, or connections well beyond what the Ground Truth contains, verified by external sources. 

• Exceeds Ground Truth: Provides some additional correct details or nuances beyond the Ground Truth. 

• Matches Ground Truth: Captures essentially the same information as the Ground Truth with comparable accuracy. 

• Falls Short of Ground Truth: Misses some details or contains minor inaccuracies compared to the Ground Truth. 

• Significantly Falls Short of Ground Truth: Major omissions, critical errors, or hallucinations com-pared to the Ground Truth. Q13. Model A vs. Model B (Head-to-Head). Comparing Model A and Model B directly, which provides a more useful, accurate, and scientifically sound explanation? 

• Model A is Much Better: Model B has critical errors or hallucinations that Model A avoids. 

• Model A is Slightly Better: Model A is more specific, complete, or has better reasoning. 

• Tie: Both are equally correct (or equally flawed). 

• Model B is Slightly Better: Model B is more specific, complete, or has better reasoning. 

• Model B is Much Better: Model A has critical errors or hallucinations that Model B avoids. 

Part III: Qualitative Critique (Free Text) 

Q14. Key Strengths. What did the model do well? (e.g. accurate function prediction, insightful domain interpretation, correct mechanistic reasoning, novel but plausible hypotheses.) Q15. Key Weaknesses. Where did the model fall short? (e.g. missed obvious features, overly generic predictions, shallow reasoning.) Q16. Errors and Hallucinations. Did the model make any factual errors or fabricate information? If so, describe specifically what was incorrect and whether it was a minor inaccuracy or a critical error that undermines the overall prediction. Q17. Reasoning Quality. Was the logical chain from evidence to conclusion sound? Were there any non-sequiturs or unjustified leaps? Q18. Comparison to Expert Expectation. How does this output compare to what you would expect from a trained biocurator or domain expert analyzing the same input? Q19. Additional Comments. Any other observations, concerns, or feedback about this case? 

Part IV: Meta-Evaluation 

Q20. Case Study Nomination. Do you find this case interesting enough to be featured as a main case study in the paper? [Yes / No] 

Evaluator Confidence. How confident are you in your evaluation of this case? [1–10]

C.5. Error Attribution for Human Evaluations 

Prompt for GPT-5-mini You are classifying expert evaluations of two protein function prediction models. Model A = SFT model, Model B = RL model. The expert was asked about errors/hallucinations. Classify errors for EACH model separately: 

• none = no errors mentioned for that model 

• minor = small inaccuracies, nitpicks, slight errors 

• major = critical errors, hallucinations, fabricated information, wrong mechanisms 

• If text discusses errors without specifying which model, mark both as the stated severity 

• If text says “no errors” or equivalent, mark both as “none”

C.6. eEFSec (P57772) Full Reasoning Trace (SFT) 

BioReason-Pro SFT Generation for eEFSec (P57772) <think> I begin with the InterPro architecture. The N-terminus (residues 5–217) carries IPR000795 (Transla-tional tr-type GTP-binding domain), embedded within the broader P-loop NTPase scaffold IPR027417 (P-loop containing nucleoside triphosphate hydrolase, residues 5–239). This arrangement establishes a translational GTPase core with the canonical P-loop NTP-binding fold and switch regions that couple GTP hydrolysis to conformational change. Immediately downstream, residues 216–306 map to IPR009000 (Translation protein, beta-barrel domain superfamily) and residues 238–301 to IPR004161 (Translation elongation factor EFTu-like, domain 2). Together these define the EF-Tu-like domain II beta-barrel that, in translation factors, forms part of the tRNA- and factor-binding platform and transmits the nucleotide state of the GTPase to the C-terminal body. The mid-to-C-terminal half then adds two specificity de-terminants unique to selenocysteine delivery: IPR049393 (Selenocysteine-specific elongation factor, 3rd domain, residues 317–448) and IPR049394 (Selenocysteine-specific elongation factor, C-terminal RIFT domain, residues 465–590). The RIFT domain is a hallmark that creates the binding surface for the selenocysteine-specific tRNA and its escorting SECIS-binding protein, thereby enforcing substrate selec-tion for selenocysteyl-tRNA. The entire polypeptide is classified within IPR050055 (Elongation factor Tu GTPase family, residues 5–445), which situates the protein within the translational GTPase lineage that powers tRNA delivery to the ribosome. This domain layout causes a precise molecular function. The tr-type GTP-binding domain and P-loop core (IPR000795 within IPR027417) confer GTPase activity (GO:0003924), with GTP binding and hydrolysis driving conformational cycling. The EF-Tu-like domain II (IPR004161) and the translation beta-barrel superfamily (IPR009000) create the structural cradle for tRNA engagement, supporting translation factor activity, RNA binding (GO:0008135) and general protein binding (GO:0005515) to assemble with partner factors. The SECIS-dependent RIFT module (IPR049393 and IPR049394) specializes this machinery to recognize selenocysteinyl-tRNA(Sec), establishing translation elongation factor activity, selenocysteine-containing tRNA binding (GO:0003748) as the defining action. From these molecular activities, the biological process follows. A GTP-driven, SECIS-dependent tRNA-recognition module that docks to the ribosome is built to operate during the decoding phase of protein synthesis. By delivering Sec-tRNA(Sec) to the A site in a codon-dependent manner, the factor executes translation elongation (GO:0006414) and, because the cycle is triggered by a stop codon being read as Sec, it functions within translational frameshifting, codon-dependent frameshifting (GO:0002185). The GTPase cycle times the productive placement of the Sec-charged tRNA and enforces fidelity by rejecting non-cognate tRNAs. The cellular context is dictated by where decoding occurs. The absence of transmembrane segments and the translational GTPase architecture indicate a soluble factor that operates on cytosolic ribosomes. Thus, the protein resides in the cytoplasm (GO:0005737), specifically the cytosol (GO:0005829), and engages the ribosome (GO:0005840) during elongation. Mechanistically, the N-terminal GTPase domain binds GTP and, in the GTP state, the EF-Tu-like domain II and C-terminal RIFT surfaces clamp onto Sec-tRNA(Sec) in conjunction with its escorting SECIS-binding protein. The complex docks to the A site of a ribosome engaged at a UGA stop codon within a SECIS-programmed context. GTP hydrolysis then triggers conformational changes that release the factor and commit the Sec-tRNA to peptidyl transfer, after which GDP-bound factor dissociates and is recycled. This model predicts interactions with SECIS-binding protein 2 (to load Sec-tRNA(Sec)), with eEF1B guanine nucleotide-exchange factors (to regenerate the active GTP-bound state), with ribosomal small-subunit proteins at the A site, and with the charged tRNA(Sec) itself. These contacts collectively enable selective, codon-dependent delivery of selenocysteine during elongation. </think>

BioReason-Pro RL Generation for eEFSec (P57772) <think> I begin by dissecting the InterPro architecture and its order along the polypeptide. The N-terminus (residues 5–217) is defined by IPR000795 (Translational tr-type GTP-binding domain), embedded within IPR027417 (P-loop containing nucleoside triphosphate hydrolase homologous superfamily, residues 5– 239). This domain architecture dictates molecular function. The N-terminal tr-type P-loop module (IPR000795 within IPR027417) causes GTP binding and hydrolysis, which defines GO:0003924 GTPase activity and supports GO:0005525 GTP binding. Cellular location is implied by function and architecture. 1) Detectable motifs / domain clues in the sequence

∼100–110) matches the hallmark NKXD motif seen in GTP-binding proteins (again consistent with septins).

Several low-complexity / Ser/Pro/Glu-rich stretches suggest regulatory regions (often phosphorylation sites). Expected InterPro (if annotated): “Septin-type GTPase domain”, “P-loop NTPase (TRAFAC class)”, “Coiled-coil region”, possibly “Septin unique element (SUE)” in the C-terminal half.

5) Protein–protein interactions (PPI) expectations

Frequently connect to actin-associated cytokinetic scaffolds (e.g., anillin-like organizers) and can interface indirectly with actin/mem-brane systems. This scaffold would couple to radial spoke and nexin–dynein regulatory complexes to stabilize inter-doublet linkages and tune dynein activity, thereby preserving beat fidelity. Putting these elements together, I hypothesize a dual-function cytosolic enzyme-scaffold. BioReason-Pro RL Generation for EvoAcr2 ( Escherichia coli )<think> I begin with the InterPro architecture. The sequence carries a single annotated module: GO:0005515 protein binding. The absence of catalytic InterPro signatures and the presence of a sole binding-centric annotation indicate a non-enzymatic adaptor or scaffold that exerts function through multivalent protein– protein interactions. Such an architecture causes activity to be mediated by assembly and remodeling of macromolecular complexes rather than by direct catalysis. From this binding-centric core, the molecular function resolves as protein binding (GO:0005515). A bind-ing scaffold of this kind can couple to diverse partners—transcriptional regulators, nucleic-acid-associated assemblies, and membrane-proximal adaptors—allowing it to tune gene expression and stress responses indirectly. This binding-driven mechanism explains two connected biological processes. First, suppression by virus of host transcription (GO:0039657) emerges mechanistically from a scaffold that can impede transcriptional programs by sequestering or reorganizing transcriptional machinery and RNA-processing assemblies. In bacteriophage infections, such host-suppression functions are co-opted or mimicked by phage-encoded factors that dampen host transcription; a non-enzymatic binder is well-suited to enforce this blockade by nucleating inhibitory complexes. Second, modulation by virus of host transcription (GO:0019056) gener-alizes this role across infection contexts, where viral or phage-associated scaffolds reshape host transcrip-tional outputs. In Escherichia coli , the same binding logic plausibly restrains endogenous transcription when stress pathways are engaged. Cellular localization follows from the same architecture. A soluble, non-membranous binder is expected to reside in the cytoplasm (GO:0005737), where it can assemble and regulate large ribonucleoprotein and transcription-associated assemblies. The additional presence in the nucleus (GO:0005634) is consis-tent with a nucleoid-proximal, chromatin-adjacent environment in bacteria—functionally analogous to a nuclear compartment—where a binding scaffold can access and modulate transcriptional hubs. These deductions converge on a mechanistic hypothesis: the protein acts as a cytoplasmic–nucleoid scaf-fold that tunes stress-responsive pathways and phage-influenced transcriptional states by dynamically binding transcription factors, RNA polymerase-associated regulators, and stress-sensing modules. Likely partners include ATP-dependent proteostasis and stress-response hubs (e.g., ClpXP), chaperone systems (DnaK/DnaJ), and transcriptional assemblies (sigma factors and RNA polymerase-associated regulators). Through these interactions, the scaffold can inhibit or reroute transcriptional flux, thereby coupling en-vironmental stress to changes in gene expression and, when applicable, to viral/host transcriptional sup-pression. </think> Functional Summary: A soluble binding scaffold in Escherichia coli that modulates gene expression and stress responses by assembling and remodeling multiprotein complexes. Its non-enzymatic architecture enables it to tune transcriptional outputs and stress-adaptation pathways in the cytoplasm and at nucleoid-proximal sites, where it likely enforces or mediates suppression of host transcription during infection and environmental challenge. This architecture causes the protein to function primarily via binding-driven assembly or sequestration of other macromolecules. From this binding-centric core, the molecular function resolves as GO:0005515 protein binding. Abinding-only scaffold can modulate transcription indirectly by tethering or occluding regulatory factors. In a bacteriophage context, such scaffolds often rewire host pathways by concentrating or exporting host proteins. The absence of enzymatic motifs and the expectation of multivalent contacts together argue that the protein’s primary role is to organize complexes that alter gene expression outputs. This binding-driven mechanism naturally links to the biological process of GO:0039657 suppression by virus of host gene expression. By assembling or sequestering host transcriptional regulators, the pro-tein can dampen or redirect host transcriptional programs—an effect that supports viral takeover of cel-lular machinery. The same logic extends to GO:0052026 modulation by virus of host transcription: a virus-encoded adaptor that binds host factors can shift promoter occupancy, nucleic acid accessibility, or chromatin-associated assemblies to favor viral fitness over host physiology. The lack of transmembrane segments and signal peptides supports a cytosolic dispo-sition. This reasoning aligns with GO:0030430 host cell cytoplasm and explains how cytoplasmic sequestration and complex assembly lead to downstream transcriptional suppression. Mechanistically, I hypothesize that the protein forms a cytoplasmic hub that captures host RNA-binding proteins and transcriptional regulators, thereby reducing their effective concentration at promoters and enhancing viral resource acquisition. Likely partners include host translation and RNA granule compo-nents (e.g., polyribosomes and RNA-binding proteins) that govern mRNA fate, as well as transcriptional modulators whose availability dictates host gene expression. I begin with the InterPro architecture. The sequence carries a single annotated module: GO:0005515 protein binding as a basal capability. The absence of catalytic InterPro signatures and the presence of a generic binding annotation point toward a non-enzymatic regulator that operates through multivalent protein–protein interactions rather than direct catalysis. Such architectures typically stabilize transcrip-tional assemblies or recruit corepressors. From this binding-centric core, I infer molecular function first. A protein whose primary capability is pro-tein binding and lacks enzymatic motifs is well-suited to assemble or modulate transcriptional complexes. This causes a regulatory rather than catalytic role, consistent with GO:0005515 protein binding as the operative molecular function that enables higher-order assembly and repression. This binding-driven regulatory role naturally extends to biological process. A binding scaffold that tunes transcriptional assemblies aligns with negative control of gene expression. The mechanistic consequence of assembling repressor complexes is the attenuation of RNA polymerase II-driven transcription, matching GO:0045892 negative regulation of DNA-templated transcription. The stated role in testis development and spermatogenesis integrates with this logic: germ cell differentiation requires waves of transcriptional repression and activation, and a cytosolic-to-nuclear shuttling regulator can time these transitions. Cellular location follows from both function and evidence. The GO cellular component leaf GO:0005829 cytosol indicates a soluble pool. Many transcriptional regulators cycle through the cytosol before nu-clear engagement; a cytosolic reservoir enables rapid deployment and fine-tuned availability. The lack of transmembrane or organelle-targeting domains supports a soluble cytosolic localization with potential nucleocytoplasmic trafficking. Putting these elements together yields a mechanistic hypothesis. A cytosolic binding scaffold that neg-atively regulates transcription likely operates by sequestering or delivering corepressor machinery to chromatin-bound transcription factors during spermatogenic stages. It may bind transcriptional repres-sors and chromatin-modifying enzymes in the cytosol, then assemble repression complexes that dampen lineage-inappropriate gene programs. In human germ cells, this could gate the switch between prolifer-ation and differentiation. From this model, I hypothesize interaction partners that enact repression and germ cell specificity. Likely associates include cytosolic corepressors and chromatin regulators such as SIN3A/SIN3B and HDAC1/HDAC2 complexes that deacetylate histones; mediator or co-repressor adaptors that shuttle be-tween cytosol and nucleus; and testis-enriched transcriptional networks (for example, factors that orches-trate germ cell gene expression). These assemblies would allow the protein to couple cytosolic storage with transcriptional silencing essential for spermatogenesis. </think> Functional Summary: A soluble human regulatory protein that relies on multivalent protein binding to assemble repression complexes controlling gene expression. By cycling through the cytosol and engaging corepressor machinery, it dampens transcriptional programs that must be silenced during testis develop-ment and spermatogenesis. Its mechanism centers on scaffolding interactions that modulate the availabil-ity and assembly of transcriptional repressors, thereby timing the switch between germ cell proliferation and differentiation.
